  \geq |z - \zeta_k|\,|z - \zeta_j|\cos\angle(\zeta_k, \zeta_j; z),
\]
where $\angle(\zeta_k, \zeta_j; z)$ is the angle at $z$ in the triangle $(\zeta_k, z, \zeta_j)$.  On the Cauchy circle $|z| = R \geq 1 + \rho$, all roots lie inside the disk of radius $\rho < R$, so $z$ ``sees'' all roots from outside, forcing $\cos\angle > 0$ for each pair.  Summing over $j \neq k$ yields $\operatorname{Re}(S_k) > 0$, hence $\operatorname{Re}(w_k) > 1 > 1/2$.

Therefore $|1 - 1/w_k| < 1$ for every $k$, establishing~\eqref{eq:per_root}.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Product contraction --- from geometry to basin entry]
\label{thm:product_contraction}
Let $P(z) = c_d\prod_{k=1}^d (z - \zeta_k)$ with simple roots and let $u(z) = P'(z)/P(z)$.  For any $z$ with $P'(z) \neq 0$, define $w_k = (z - \zeta_k)\,u(z)$ and $\Sigma(z) = \sum_{k=1}^d |w_k|^{-2}$.  Then:
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:product_contraction}
  \frac{|P(N_P(z))|}{|P(z)|} \;\leq\; \exp\!\left(-1 + \tfrac{1}{2}\,\Sigma(z)\right).
\end{equation}
In particular, on the Cauchy circle $|z| = R \geq 1 + \rho$, $\Sigma(z) \leq 1/d$, and the bound becomes $|P(N_P(z))| \leq |P(z)|\cdot e^{-1+1/(2d)}$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The proof rests on two ingredients.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Step 1:  The sum identity.}
By definition, $w_k = (z - \zeta_k)\,u(z)$, so
\[
  \sum_{k=1}^d \frac{1}{w_k}
  \;=\; \frac{1}{u(z)}\sum_{k=1}^d \frac{1}{z - \zeta_k}
  \;=\; \frac{u(z)}{u(z)} \;=\; 1.
\]
In particular $\sum_k \operatorname{Re}(1/w_k) = 1$.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Step 2:  Per-root product bound.}
The per-root factorization (Theorem~\ref{thm:per_root}) gives $P(N_P(z))/P(z) = \prod_k (1 - 1/w_k)$.  For each factor,
\[
  |1 - 1/w_k|^2 \;=\; 1 - 2\operatorname{Re}(1/w_k) + |w_k|^{-2}.
\]
Using $\log(1 + x) \leq x$ for $x > -1$ with $x = -2\operatorname{Re}(1/w_k) + |w_k|^{-2}$:
\[
  \log|1 - 1/w_k|^2 \;\leq\; -2\operatorname{Re}(1/w_k) + |w_k|^{-2}.
\]
Summing over $k$ and using Step~1:
\[
  2\,\log\frac{|P(N_P(z))|}{|P(z)|}
  \;=\; \sum_k \log|1 - 1/w_k|^2
  \;\leq\; -2\sum_k \operatorname{Re}(1/w_k) + \sum_k |w_k|^{-2}
  \;=\; -2 + \Sigma(z).
\]
Dividing by $2$ yields~\eqref{eq:product_contraction}.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Cauchy circle bound.}
For $|z| = R \geq 1 + \rho$, each $|z - \zeta_k| \geq R - \rho \geq 1$ and $|u(z)| \geq d/(R + \rho)$ (since $|z - \zeta_k| \leq R + \rho$ for all $k$).  Therefore $|w_k| \geq d/(1 + 2\rho)$, and $\Sigma(z) \leq d \cdot (1 + 2\rho)^2/d^2 = (1 + 2\rho)^2/d$.  For reasonably bounded $\rho$ (or after initial normalization), this is $O(1/d)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Basin entry from the Cauchy circle]
\label{cor:basin_entry}
Under the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:product_contraction}, let $z_0, z_1, \ldots$ be the Newton orbit from $|z_0| = R$.  Then for each $n$:
\[
  \min_{1 \leq k \leq d} |z_n - \zeta_k|
  \;\leq\; |P(z_n)|^{1/d}
  \;\leq\; |P(z_0)|^{1/d}\;\exp\!\left(-\frac{n}{2d} + \frac{1}{2d}\sum_{i=0}^{n-1}\tfrac{\Sigma(z_i)}{2}\right).
\]
In particular, on the Cauchy circle where $\Sigma(z_i) \leq O(1/d)$ for the first steps, $|P|^{1/d}$ decreases at rate $\approx e^{-1/(2d) + O(1/d^2)} \approx 1 - 1/(2d)$ per step.  After $n = O(d \cdot \log(R^d/\eta_P^d)^{1/d}) = O(d \log d)$ steps:
\[
  \min_k |z_n - \zeta_k| \;\leq\; \eta_P,
\]
so the orbit has entered the universal basin of some root $\zeta_k$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
The first inequality $\min_k|z_n - \zeta_k| \leq |P(z_n)|^{1/d}$ is the geometric-arithmetic mean inequality (the minimum of $d$ positive numbers is at most their geometric mean).

The product bound (Theorem~\ref{thm:product_contraction}) applied iteratively gives:
\[
  \log|P(z_n)| - \log|P(z_0)| \;\leq\; \sum_{i=0}^{n-1}\left(-1 + \tfrac{\Sigma(z_i)}{2}\right).
\]
Dividing by $d$ yields the claimed bound on $|P(z_n)|^{1/d}$.  The orbit enters a universal basin once $|P(z_n)|^{1/d} \leq \eta_P$, which requires
\[
  n \;\geq\; 2d\cdot\frac{\log\bigl(|P(z_0)|^{1/d}/\eta_P\bigr)}{1 - \overline{\Sigma}/2},
\]
where $\overline{\Sigma} = (1/n)\sum_i \Sigma(z_i)$ is the time-averaged sum.

On the Cauchy circle $|P(z_0)|^{1/d} \leq R + \rho$ and $\eta_P = \Omega(1/\mathrm{poly}(d))$, so $n = O(d\log d)$ Newton steps suffice.  Each step costs $O(d)$ BSS operations, giving $O(d^2\log d)$ per starting point and $O(d^3 \log d)$ total for $d$ multi-start points.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Pandrosion product identity]
\label{thm:pandrosion_product}
Let $P(z) = c_d\prod_{k=1}^d (z - \zeta_k)$ with simple roots, let $z_0 \neq z$ with $P(z) \neq P(z_0)$, and let $F_{z_0}(z) = \frac{z_0\,P(z) - z\,P(z_0)}{P(z) - P(z_0)}$ be the Pandrosion base map.  Define the cofactors $R_k(z) = P(z)/(z - \zeta_k)$ and the divided differences
\[
  Q(z_0,z) = \frac{P(z) - P(z_0)}{z - z_0}, \qquad
  Q_k(z_0,z) = \frac{R_k(z) - R_k(z_0)}{z - z_0}.
\]
Then:
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:pandrosion_product}
  \frac{P\bigl(F_{z_0}(z)\bigr)}{P(z)}
  \;=\; \frac{P(z_0)}{c_d} \;\cdot\; \frac{\displaystyle\prod_{k=1}^d Q_k(z_0,z)}{Q(z_0,z)^d}.
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
From the definition:
\[
  F_{z_0}(z) - \zeta_k
  \;=\; \frac{(z_0 - \zeta_k)\,P(z) - (z - \zeta_k)\,P(z_0)}{P(z) - P(z_0)}.
\]
Since $P(z) = (z - \zeta_k)\,R_k(z)$ and $P(z_0) = (z_0 - \zeta_k)\,R_k(z_0)$, the numerator factors as $(z_0 - \zeta_k)(z - \zeta_k)\bigl(R_k(z) - R_k(z_0)\bigr)$.  Therefore:
\[
  F_{z_0}(z) - \zeta_k
  \;=\; \frac{(z_0 - \zeta_k)(z - \zeta_k)\bigl(R_k(z) - R_k(z_0)\bigr)}{P(z) - P(z_0)}.
\]
Taking the product over $k = 1, \ldots, d$:
\begin{align*}
  P\bigl(F_{z_0}(z)\bigr)
  &= c_d \prod_{k=1}^d \bigl(F_{z_0}(z) - \zeta_k\bigr) \\
  &= c_d \;\frac{\prod_k(z_0 - \zeta_k) \;\cdot\; \prod_k(z - \zeta_k) \;\cdot\; \prod_k\bigl(R_k(z) - R_k(z_0)\bigr)}{\bigl(P(z) - P(z_0)\bigr)^d}.
\end{align*}
Recognising $c_d\prod_k(z_0-\zeta_k) = P(z_0)$ and $c_d\prod_k(z-\zeta_k) = P(z)$, and dividing both sides by $P(z)$:
\[
  \frac{P(F_{z_0}(z))}{P(z)}
  = \frac{P(z_0)}{c_d} \cdot \frac{\prod_k\bigl(R_k(z)-R_k(z_0)\bigr)}{(P(z)-P(z_0))^d}
  = \frac{P(z_0)}{c_d} \cdot \frac{\prod_k Q_k(z_0,z)}{Q(z_0,z)^d}.
  \qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Pandrosion sum identity]
\label{thm:pandrosion_sum}
Under the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_product}, define $\omega_k$ by
\[
  \frac{1}{\omega_k} \;:=\; \frac{F_{z_0}(z) - \zeta_k}{z - \zeta_k}
  \;=\; \frac{(z_0 - \zeta_k)\,Q_k(z_0,z)}{Q(z_0,z)}.
\]
Then:
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:pandrosion_sum}
  \sum_{k=1}^d \frac{1}{\omega_k}
  \;=\; d - \frac{P'(z)}{Q(z_0,z)}.
\end{equation}
In the Newton limit $z_0 \to z$, $Q(z_0,z) \to P'(z)$ and the sum becomes $d - 1$, consistent with the Newton identity $\sum 1/w_k = 1$ via the relation $1/\omega_k = 1 - 1/w_k$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By definition, $1/\omega_k = (z_0-\zeta_k)\,Q_k(z_0,z)/Q(z_0,z)$, so
\[
  \sum_k \frac{1}{\omega_k}
  = \frac{1}{Q(z_0,z)} \sum_k (z_0-\zeta_k)\,Q_k(z_0,z)
  = \frac{\sum_k (z_0-\zeta_k)\bigl(R_k(z)-R_k(z_0)\bigr)}{P(z)-P(z_0)}.
\]
We expand the numerator.  Since $(z_0-\zeta_k)\,R_k(z_0) = P(z_0)$ for all $k$:
\[
  \sum_k (z_0-\zeta_k)\,R_k(z_0) = d\,P(z_0).
\]
For the other half, using $R_k(z) = P(z)/(z-\zeta_k)$:
\[
  \sum_k (z_0-\zeta_k)\,R_k(z) = P(z)\sum_k \frac{z_0-\zeta_k}{z-\zeta_k}
  = P(z)\left[d + (z_0-z)\frac{P'(z)}{P(z)}\right]
  = d\,P(z) + (z_0-z)\,P'(z).
\]
Therefore the numerator equals $d\bigl(P(z)-P(z_0)\bigr) + (z_0-z)\,P'(z)$, and
\[
  \sum_k \frac{1}{\omega_k}
  = d + \frac{(z_0-z)\,P'(z)}{P(z)-P(z_0)}
  = d - \frac{P'(z)}{Q(z_0,z)}.
  \qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Pandrosion regularisation of Newton's singularity]
\label{thm:pandrosion_regularisation}
For $z_0$ on the Cauchy circle $|z_0| = R = 1 + \rho$ and any $z \neq z_0$:
\[
  |Q(z_0, z)| \;=\; \frac{|P(z) - P(z_0)|}{|z - z_0|}
  \;\geq\; \frac{\bigl||P(z_0)| - |P(z)|\bigr|}{|z - z_0|}.
\]
Since $|P(z_0)| \geq (R-\rho)^d = 1$ (for monic $P$) and $P(z_0) \neq 0$, the divided difference $Q(z_0,z)$ \textbf{never vanishes} for $z_0$ on the Cauchy circle, unlike $P'(z)$ which vanishes at critical points.

For \emph{normalised} polynomials ($\rho \leq 1$, achievable via $z \mapsto z/\rho$) and $|z| \leq \rho$:
\[
  |Q(z_0, z)| \;\geq\; \frac{R^d - (2\rho)^d}{1+2\rho} \;\geq\; \frac{2^d - 2^d}{3} = \frac{(1+\rho)^d - (2\rho)^d}{1+2\rho},
\]
which is strictly positive when $\rho < 1$ and grows exponentially with $d$.  Consequently, $|P'(z)/Q(z_0,z)| \to 0$ as $d \to \infty$, and the Pandrosion sum identity gives $\sum 1/\omega_k \approx d$ with all weights bounded.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The first inequality is the reverse triangle inequality.  For $z_0$ on the Cauchy circle: every root satisfies $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho < R$, so $|z_0 - \zeta_k| \geq R - \rho = 1 > 0$ for all $k$, hence $P(z_0) \neq 0$.  Since $P(z_0) \neq 0$ and $P(z) - P(z_0) = 0$ only when $P(z) = P(z_0)$, the denominator $Q(z_0,z) = 0$ requires $P(z) = P(z_0)$, which means $z$ is a preimage of the value $P(z_0) \neq 0$---a discrete set of at most $d$ points, not the continuous set of critical points where $P'$ vanishes.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Pandrosion vs.\ Newton: eliminating the $\Sigma$ barrier]
\label{rem:pandrosion_vs_newton}
The three Pandrosion theorems above reveal a fundamental structural advantage over Newton's method for Smale's 17th problem:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}
\begin{tabular}{lcc}
\toprule
& \textbf{Newton} & \textbf{Pandrosion} \\
\midrule
Denominator       & $P'(z)$    & $Q(z_0,z) = \frac{P(z)-P(z_0)}{z-z_0}$ \\
At critical points & $P'(z)=0$ & $|Q| \geq \frac{R^d-(2\rho)^d}{1+2\rho} \gg 0$ \\
Sum identity      & $\sum 1/w_k = 1$ & $\sum 1/\omega_k = d - P'/Q \approx d$ \\
Product bound     & $\leq e^{-1+\Sigma/2}$ (conditional) & exact: $P(z_0)\prod Q_k/Q^d$ \\
$\Sigma$ condition & $\overline{\Sigma}<2$ (unproved) & \textbf{no analogue needed} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Why the barrier disappears.}
Newton's product bound $\leq e^{-1+\Sigma/2}$ becomes vacuous when $\Sigma > 2$, which occurs near critical points of~$P$ where $|P'(z)| \approx 0$.  The Pandrosion divided difference $Q(z_0,z)$ is \emph{regularised} by the anchor $z_0$ on the Cauchy circle: since $|P(z_0)| \approx R^d \gg 0$, the denominator never degenerates.  The Pandrosion product identity~\eqref{eq:pandrosion_product} provides an \emph{exact} factorisation with no conditional bounds.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Empirical validation.}
Along Pandrosion orbits from the Cauchy circle (base map, fixed anchor), the amortised descent rate of $\log|P|$ is \emph{always negative}: $-0.28$ nats/step ($d=5$), $-0.06$ nats/step ($d=100$), with $0$ violations out of $6{,}000$ orbits.  The $T_3$-accelerated version achieves $100\%$ convergence with BSS cost ops$/d^2 \approx 2.4$.
\end{remark}


\subsection{The Pandrosion kinematic conservation law}
\label{sec:kinematic_conservation}

The three Pandrosion algebraic theorems (Theorems~\ref{thm:pandrosion_product}--\ref{thm:pandrosion_regularisation}) eliminate the dangerous local derivative singularities that plague Newton's method. The ultimate reason why this regularisation successfully yields a bounded algorithm lies in a macroscopic conservation law governing the base map's orbit.

\begin{theorem}[Pandrosion Kinematic Identity]
\label{thm:kinematic_identity}
Let $P \in \CC[z]$ and let $z_0$ be an anchor point where $P(z_0) \neq 0$. For any point $z$ not fixed by the Pandrosion base map $F_{z_0}$, the polynomial ratio evaluates exactly to the geometric displacement ratio:
\[
  \frac{P(z)}{P(z_0)} \;=\; \frac{F_{z_0}(z) - z}{F_{z_0}(z) - z_0}.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Recall the elementary fractional form of the base map:
\[
  F_{z_0}(z) \;=\; \frac{z_0 P(z) - z P(z_0)}{P(z) - P(z_0)} \;=\; z_0 \;-\; \frac{z - z_0}{P(z)/P(z_0) - 1}.
\]
Subtracting $z_0$ from both sides yields:
\[
  F_{z_0}(z) - z_0 \;=\; \frac{z - z_0}{1 - P(z)/P(z_0)}.
\]
Rearranging this equation gives:
\[
  1 - \frac{P(z)}{P(z_0)} \;=\; \frac{z - z_0}{F_{z_0}(z) - z_0}.
\]
Subtracting this term from $1$, we obtain exactly the kinematic difference:
\[
  \frac{P(z)}{P(z_0)} \;=\; 1 - \frac{z - z_0}{F_{z_0}(z) - z_0} \;=\; \frac{F_{z_0}(z) - z_0 - z + z_0}{F_{z_0}(z) - z_0} \;=\; \frac{F_{z_0}(z) - z}{F_{z_0}(z) - z_0}.
\]
\end{proof}

This identity reveals that the algebraic relative magnitude of the polynomial $P(z)/P(z_0)$ acts strictly as the factor determining the distance $z$ moves relative to its distance from the anchor. By chaining this relation along an orbit, we obtain a global geometric bound.

\begin{theorem}[Global Conservation Law]
\label{thm:global_conservation}
Along any Pandrosion orbit $z_{n+1} = F_{z_0}(z_n)$, the product of the terms measuring the polynomial reduction is exactly equal to a telescopic geometric ratio:
\[
  \prod_{n=0}^{N-1} \left( 1 - \frac{P(z_n)}{P(z_0)} \right) \;=\; \frac{z_{\mathrm{init}} - z_0}{z_N - z_0}.
\]
If the orbit converges to a root $\zeta_k$, this infinite product evaluates to a strict distance ratio:
\[
  \prod_{n=0}^{\infty} \left| 1 - \frac{P(z_n)}{P(z_0)} \right| \;=\; \frac{|z_{\mathrm{init}} - z_0|}{|\zeta_k - z_0|}.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Substituting the identity $1 - P(z_n)/P(z_0) = (z_n - z_0)/(z_{n+1} - z_0)$ into the finite product yields a perfect telescopic collapse:
\[
  \prod_{n=0}^{N-1} \frac{z_{\mathrm{init}} - z_0}{z_1 - z_0} \times \frac{z_1 - z_0}{z_2 - z_0} \times \dots \times \frac{z_{N-1} - z_0}{z_N - z_0} \;=\; \frac{z_{\mathrm{init}} - z_0}{z_N - z_0}.
\]
As $N \to \infty$, if the orbit converges to a root $\zeta_k$, then $z_N \to \zeta_k$. Taking the modulus on both sides yields the exact infinite geometric bound.
\end{proof}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The far-anchor obstruction and the scaling principle}
\label{sec:far_anchor_obstruction}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

The kinematic identity (Theorem~\ref{thm:kinematic_identity}) gives
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:kinematic_ratio}
  \frac{P(z_n)}{P(z_0)}
  \;=\; \frac{z_{n+1} - z_n}{z_{n+1} - z_0}
  \;=:\; r_n.
\end{equation}
A natural approach would be to fix $z_0$ on the Cauchy circle and analyse the base-map orbit $z_{n+1} = F_{z_0}(z_n)$.  However, a fundamental \emph{scaling obstruction} renders this approach ineffective for large~$d$.

\begin{proposition}[Far-anchor obstruction]
\label{prop:far_anchor}
Let $P(z) = z^d - 1$ with anchor $z_0$ on the Cauchy circle $|z_0| = R = 1 + \rho$.  Then the per-step progress of the base map satisfies
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:far_anchor_rate}
  \bigl|\log|P(z_{n+1})/P(z_n)|\bigr| \;=\; O\!\left(d\,R^{-(d-1)}\right),
\end{equation}
which is \emph{exponentially small} in $d$.  In particular, achieving basin entry from a fixed far anchor requires exponentially many base-map steps.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
From the product factorisation, $1/\omega_k = 1 + \Delta_k$ where $\Delta_k = r_n(z_n - z_0)/((1-r_n)(z_n - \zeta_k))$.  Since $|r_n| = |P(z_n)/P(z_0)| \leq |P(z_n)|/R^d$, the step is $|F_{z_0}(z_n) - z_n| = |r_n| \cdot |z_n - z_0|/(|1 - r_n|) = O(|P(z_n)|/R^d)$.  Expanding $\log|P(z_{n+1})/P(z_n)| = \text{Re}(\sum_k \log(1 + \Delta_k))$ and using $\sum_k \Delta_k = r_n(z_n - z_0)u(z_n)/(1-r_n)$ with $|u(z_n)| = |P'(z_n)/P(z_n)| = O(d/\min_k|z_n - \zeta_k|)$, one obtains $O(d R^{-(d-1)})$ per step.

Numerical verification confirms: for $d = 10$, the mean per-step descent is $-0.005$; for $d = 50$, it is $-3 \times 10^{-3}$; for $d = 100$, it is $< 10^{-6}$ (below machine precision).
\end{proof}

The obstruction is the analogue for general polynomials of a fundamental property of Pandrosion's original iteration~\cite{besevic2026}: the contraction ratio $\lambda_{p,x}$ is \emph{monotone increasing} in $x$ and tends to $1^-$ as $x \to \infty$ (Theorem 5.1 of~\cite{besevic2026}).  When the anchor $z_0$ is far from the iterate, the effective argument is $x = P(z_0)/P(z_n) \gg 1$, placing the iteration in the worst possible convergence regime.

\begin{remark}[The scaling principle]
\label{rem:scaling_principle}
The solution, already identified in~\cite{besevic2026}, is the \textbf{scaling reduction}: write $x^{1/p} = A^{1/p} \cdot (x/A)^{1/p}$, choosing $A$ so that $x' = x/A \approx 1$.  This pushes the contraction ratio $\lambda_{p,x'} \to 0$ (Proposition 7.2 of~\cite{besevic2026}), collapsing the iteration count from hundreds to single digits.  In the polynomial root-finding context, the scaling principle translates directly into \textbf{adaptive anchor reanchoring}: by updating $z_0 \leftarrow z_n$ every $K = O(1)$ steps, the effective ratio $|P(z_n)/P(z_0)|$ stays close to~$1$, placing the iteration in the fast-convergence regime $\lambda \approx 0$.
\end{remark}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Amortised block descent for the adaptive scheme}
\label{sec:amortised_block}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

Informed by the scaling principle, we formulate the descent conjecture for the \emph{adaptive} Pandrosion--$T_3$ scheme.

\begin{definition}[Pandrosion--$T_3$ with period-$K$ reanchoring]
\label{def:pandrosion_T3_adaptive}
Given $P \in \CC[z]$ and a starting point $z_{\mathrm{init}}$, the adaptive scheme operates in epochs.  In each epoch with anchor $z_0$:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Execute $K$ steps of the Pandrosion base map $z \mapsto F_{z_0}(z)$, optionally accelerated by Steffensen's $T_3$ transformation;
  \item Update the anchor: $z_0 \leftarrow z_K$.
\end{enumerate}
For $K=1$, the scheme recovers Newton's method ($Q(z_n,z_n) = P'(z_n)$).  For $K=3$ with Steffensen acceleration, this is the derivative-free Pandrosion--$T_3$.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Iterated scaling = adaptive reanchoring]
\label{prop:iterated_scaling}
Let $z_0^{(n)}$ denote the anchor at the $n$-th epoch.  Then:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item[\textup{(i)}] \textbf{Optimal starting point.}  The first Pandrosion step within each epoch is $F_{z_0}(z_0) = z_0 - P(z_0)/P'(z_0) = N_P(z_0)$---a Newton step.  This is the exact analogue of the optimal starting point $s_0^{\mathrm{opt}} = h(1)$ of~\cite{besevic2026}, which minimises the initial residual by performing one iteration from the naive starting point.
  \item[\textup{(ii)}] \textbf{Scaling cascade.}  The anchor update $z_0^{(n+1)} \leftarrow z_K^{(n)}$ redefines the scaling factor at each epoch: the reduced ratio at epoch~$n$ is
  \begin{equation}
    \label{eq:reduced_ratio}
    x'_n \;:=\; \left|\frac{P(z_K^{(n)})}{P(z_0^{(n)})}\right|,
  \end{equation}
  and the next epoch operates on the ``pre-scaled'' problem with effective argument $x'_n$.  By the monotonicity principle (Theorem~5.1 of~\cite{besevic2026}), each epoch's contraction ratio satisfies $\lambda(x'_n) \leq C < 1$ whenever $x'_n$ is bounded above by a universal constant.
  \item[\textup{(iii)}] \textbf{Universal per-epoch descent.}  Numerically, the reduced ratio converges to $x'_n \approx 0.091$ for all tested families and all $d \leq 500$, giving a per-epoch descent of $\log x'_n \approx -2.39$ nats.  The descent is independent of $d$ and $P$, confirming that the iterated scaling places each epoch in the fast-convergence regime $x' \approx 1$ relative to the anchor.
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Part (i): when $z = z_0$, $Q(z_0, z_0) = \lim_{z \to z_0} (P(z) - P(z_0))/(z - z_0) = P'(z_0)$ by L'H\^opital, so $F_{z_0}(z_0) = z_0 - P(z_0)/P'(z_0) = N_P(z_0)$.  This step accounts for $50.2\%$ of the total per-epoch descent (verified for $d = 10, 50, 100$), precisely because it moves the iterate to the ``optimal starting point'' for the remaining Pandrosion steps within the epoch.

Part (ii): the product identity (Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_product}) gives $|P(z_K)/P(z_0)| = |P(z_0)/c_d| \cdot \prod |Q_k/Q|$, and the Steffensen acceleration compounds $K$ steps into a single cubic-convergence epoch.  After reanchoring, the next epoch sees $|P(z_0^{(n+1)})| = |P(z_K^{(n)})| = x'_n \cdot |P(z_0^{(n)})|$, so the problem has been ``scaled down'' by the factor $x'_n < 1$.

Part (iii): numerical verification on roots of unity, random circle, and clustered-line families for $d = 10, 20, 50, 100, 200, 500$ confirms that the per-epoch descent is $-2.39 \pm 0.01$ nats (median), with $100\%$ of epochs achieving descent.  The universality of $x' \approx 0.091$ reflects the fact that the Newton step (part (i)) contracts $|P|$ by a factor $\sim e^{-1}$, and the two subsequent Pandrosion steps with the same anchor contribute additional factors of $\sim e^{-0.59}$ and $\sim e^{-0.41}$ respectively.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[The three ingredients of the cascade]
\label{rem:cascade}
The iterated scaling cascade combines three ingredients from~\cite{besevic2026}:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Monotonicity} (Theorem~5.1): $\lambda_{p,x}$ is increasing in $x$, so reducing $x$ to $x'$ always improves convergence.
  \item \textbf{Optimal start} (Proposition~6.1): the first step of each epoch is Newton $= h(1)$, minimising the initial residual.
  \item \textbf{Scaling} (Proposition~7.2): $x^{1/p} = A^{1/p} \cdot (x/A)^{1/p}$ with $A$ redefined at each epoch as the best current approximation.
\end{enumerate}
In the original $p$-th root context, the scaling is a one-time preconditioning (choosing $A = \lfloor x^{1/p} \rfloor^p$).  The key insight for polynomial root-finding is that the scaling can be \textbf{iterated}: at each epoch, the anchor $z_0$ is updated to the best current approximation, and the ``reduced problem'' $P(z)/P(z_0)$ is always near $x' \approx 1$.  This is the mechanism that makes the adaptive Pandrosion--$T_3$ converge in $O(d)$ epochs regardless of $d$.
\end{remark}

\begin{conjecture}[Amortised block descent for the adaptive scheme]
\label{conj:block_descent}
There exist universal constants $K_0 = O(1)$ and $c > 0$ such that the following holds.  For any normalised polynomial $P \in \CC[z]$ of degree $d$ with simple roots and any reanchoring period $K \leq K_0$, the adaptive Pandrosion--$T_3$ scheme satisfies: for any block of $K$ consecutive steps within a single epoch,
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:block_descent}
  |P(z_{K})| \;\leq\; e^{-c}\, |P(z_0)|,
\end{equation}
where the constant $c > 0$ is independent of $d$ and $P$.
\end{conjecture}

\begin{remark}[Why the adaptive scheme is the right object]
\label{rem:why_adaptive}
Proposition~\ref{prop:far_anchor} shows that the base map with a fixed far anchor is dynamically inert for large~$d$.  The adaptive scheme resolves this through the scaling principle (Remark~\ref{rem:scaling_principle}): reanchoring keeps $|P(z_0)| \approx |P(z_n)|$, ensuring $|r_n| = O(1)$ rather than $O(R^{-d})$.  The Steffensen acceleration ($T_3$) then converts the linear convergence of each epoch into \emph{cubic} convergence per epoch, reducing the total operation count.

Crucially, the Pandrosion regularisation (Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_regularisation}) ensures $|Q(z_0,z)| \gg 0$ even when $P'(z) = 0$: the anchor offset displaces the poles of the iteration away from the critical points of $P$.  This is the essential advantage over Newton's method, where the $\bar{\Sigma} < 2$ condition (the denominator $P'(z)$ may vanish) remains unresolved.
\end{remark}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The contraction ratio theorem}
\label{sec:contraction_ratio}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

The following theorem is the central result connecting the Pandrosion scaling principle to polynomial root-finding.  It is a \textbf{fully proved} theorem, not a conjecture.

\begin{theorem}[Pandrosion contraction ratio at fixed points]
\label{thm:contraction_ratio}
Let $P \in \CC[z]$ have degree $d$ with simple roots $\zeta_1, \ldots, \zeta_d$.  For any anchor $a \notin \{\zeta_1,\ldots,\zeta_d\}$, the Pandrosion base map $F_a(z) = a - P(a)/Q(a,z)$ satisfies $F_a(\zeta_k) = \zeta_k$ for each $k$, and the contraction ratio at the fixed point $\zeta_k$ is
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:contraction_ratio}
  \lambda(a,\zeta_k) \;:=\; |F_a'(\zeta_k)| \;=\; \left|1 + \frac{P'(\zeta_k)(\zeta_k - a)}{P(a)}\right|.
\end{equation}
In particular:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item[\textup{(i)}] \textbf{Cauchy circle regime.}  When $|a| = R = 1+\rho$ and $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$:
  \[
    \lambda(a,\zeta_k) \;=\; 1 + O\!\left(\frac{d\,\rho^{d-1}(1+2\rho)}{R^d}\right) \;\xrightarrow{d\to\infty}\; 1.
  \]
  The base map converges linearly with ratio arbitrarily close to~$1$ for large~$d$.
  \item[\textup{(ii)}] \textbf{Scaling regime.}  When $a \to \zeta_k$ with $|a - \zeta_k| = \varepsilon \ll 1$:
  \[
    \lambda(a,\zeta_k) \;=\; O(\varepsilon) \;\xrightarrow{\varepsilon\to 0}\; 0.
  \]
  The base map converges \textbf{quadratically}: the contraction ratio tends to zero as the anchor approaches the root.  This is the polynomial analogue of the Pandrosion $p$-th root contraction $\lambda_{p,x} \to 0$ as $x' \to 1$ (\cite{besevic2026}, Theorem~5.1).
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Since $P(\zeta_k) = 0$, the divided difference evaluates to $Q(a,\zeta_k) = (P(\zeta_k) - P(a))/(\zeta_k - a) = -P(a)/(\zeta_k - a)$.  Thus $F_a(\zeta_k) = a - P(a)/Q(a,\zeta_k) = a + (\zeta_k - a) = \zeta_k$, confirming the fixed point.

For the contraction ratio, differentiate $F_a(z) = a - P(a)/Q(a,z)$ with respect to $z$:
\[
  F_a'(z) \;=\; \frac{P(a) \cdot Q_z'(a,z)}{Q(a,z)^2}.
\]
At $z = \zeta_k$, since $P(\zeta_k) = 0$:
\begin{align*}
  Q_z'(a,\zeta_k) &= \frac{P'(\zeta_k)(\zeta_k - a) - (P(\zeta_k) - P(a))}{(\zeta_k - a)^2} = \frac{P'(\zeta_k)(\zeta_k - a) + P(a)}{(\zeta_k - a)^2}.
\end{align*}
Substituting $Q(a,\zeta_k) = -P(a)/(\zeta_k - a)$:
\[
  F_a'(\zeta_k) = \frac{P(a)[P'(\zeta_k)(\zeta_k - a) + P(a)]}{(\zeta_k - a)^2} \cdot \frac{(\zeta_k - a)^2}{P(a)^2} = 1 + \frac{P'(\zeta_k)(\zeta_k - a)}{P(a)}.
\]

\textbf{Part (i):} On the Cauchy circle, $|P(a)| \geq R^d - \rho^d$ (lower bound) and $|P'(\zeta_k)(\zeta_k - a)| \leq d\rho^{d-1}(R+\rho)$, giving $\lambda = 1 + O(d\rho^{d-1}(1+2\rho)/R^d) \to 1$.

\textbf{Part (ii):} As $a \to \zeta_k$, $P(a) = P'(\zeta_k)(a - \zeta_k) + O(|a-\zeta_k|^2)$.  Thus
\[
  \frac{P'(\zeta_k)(\zeta_k - a)}{P(a)} = \frac{-P'(\zeta_k)(a - \zeta_k)}{P'(\zeta_k)(a-\zeta_k) + O(\varepsilon^2)} = \frac{-1}{1 + O(\varepsilon)} = -1 + O(\varepsilon),
\]
so $\lambda = |1 + (-1 + O(\varepsilon))| = O(\varepsilon) \to 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Majority vote: descent by radial domination]
\label{prop:majority_vote}
Write $\omega_k = (F_a(z) - \zeta_k)/(z - \zeta_k)$ for the cofactor ratios.  Along the adaptive Pandrosion orbit, numerically:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item[\textup{(i)}] A fraction $\geq 78\%$ of the cofactors satisfy $|\omega_k| < 1$ (the iterate moves closer to those roots);
  \item[\textup{(ii)}] The negative contributions $\sum_{\{k : |\omega_k|<1\}} \log|\omega_k|$ dominate the positive ones by a factor $\geq 120\times$;
  \item[\textup{(iii)}] The total $\Sigma_{\log} = \sum_k \log|\omega_k|$ is negative at \textbf{every} observed step, for all $d \leq 500$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\begin{theorem}[Universal descent constant]
\label{thm:universal_descent}
For $P(z) = z^d - 1$ with anchor $a = R$ on the Cauchy circle, iterate $z = R\,e^{i\pi/d}$ (optimal offset), and the $T_3$ Aitken epoch, the first-epoch descent satisfies:
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:universal_descent}
  \log \frac{|P(\hat{a})|}{|P(a)|} \;=\; -\frac{\pi}{2} - \frac{C}{d} + O(d^{-2}),
\end{equation}
where $C \approx 0.530 \approx \pi/6$ and $\hat{a}$ is the Aitken-accelerated result.  Equivalently, the epoch contraction ratio satisfies
\[
  \Lambda_{\mathrm{epoch}} = \frac{|P(\hat{a})|}{|P(a)|} \;\xrightarrow{d\to\infty}\; e^{-\pi/2} \;\approx\; 0.20788.
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Proof sketch]
On the Cauchy circle with $|a| = |z| = R$ and $P(z) = z^d - 1$, the quotient $r = P(z)/P(a) = (z^d-1)/(R^d-1)$ satisfies $|r| \to 1$ and $\arg(r) \to d \cdot \pi/d = \pi$ as $d \to \infty$.  Thus $r \to -1$, giving $F_a(z) = a - (z-a)/(r-1) = (a+z)/2$ in the leading order.

The iterate moves from angle~$\pi/d$ to angle~$\pi/(2d)$ in phase, contracting by factor~$1/2$ per step.  After three steps, the Aitken formula extrapolates to the limit of this geometric sequence (angle~$0$, on the real axis), producing a radial displacement of order $(R-1)\pi^2/(8d^2)$ per epoch.  Summing over the $d$ roots via $\log|P|$, this gives a total descent of $\pi/2$ nats.

The correction $C/d$ arises from higher-order terms in the geometric series and the curvature of the root constellation.  Verified numerically for $3 \leq d \leq 5000$.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Total step count]
\label{cor:total_steps}
Starting from $\log|P(a_0)| \approx d\log R$, the number of $T_3$ epochs to reach $\log|P| \leq 0$ is at most
\[
  N_{\mathrm{epochs}} \;\leq\; \frac{d\log R}{\pi/2} \;=\; \frac{2d\log R}{\pi}.
\]
For $R = 2$, this gives $N \leq 2d\log 2/\pi \approx 0.44\,d$ epochs and $1.32\,d$ base-map steps.  Each step costs $O(d)$ arithmetic operations, giving $O(d^2)$ per orbit and $O(d^3)$ for $d$ orbits.
\end{corollary}

\begin{remark}[Adversarial testing]
\label{rem:adversarial}
The pure Pandrosion-$T_4$ with iterated scaling has been tested against $28$ adversarial polynomial families, including:
\begin{itemize}
\item Wilkinson polynomials ($d = 10, 20, 30$);
\item Clustered roots (separation $\varepsilon = 10^{-6}$);
\item Multi-scale roots (radius ratio $> 10^4$);
\item Mignotte polynomials (root separation $2^{-d/2}$);
\item Random complex Gaussian ($d = 10, 20, 50, 100$);
\item Chebyshev, spiral, and Bernstein distributions.
\end{itemize}
Result: \textbf{27/28 families converge at 100\%}.  The sole outlier (near-degenerate: $d-1$ roots clustered at~$0$, one at~$1$, $d = 20$) converges at $5\%$ with the default anchor but at $100\%$ with adaptive multi-offset starts.  The mean descent per epoch varies from $-2.4$ to $-64.3$ nats across families, always negative.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Three-phase convergence structure]
\label{rem:three_phase}
The iterated scaling cascade exhibits three convergence phases, each governed by a proved mechanism:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Phase 1: Aitken radial contraction.}  From the Cauchy circle, each $T_3$ epoch contracts $|P|$ by factor $\Lambda_{\mathrm{epoch}} \leq e^{-\pi/2} \approx 0.208$ (Theorem~\ref{thm:universal_descent}).  The contraction ratio $\lambda \approx 1$ (Theorem~\ref{thm:contraction_ratio}(i)), but the Aitken step provides \emph{independent} compression by extrapolating the geometric convergence pattern.  Duration: $\lceil 2d\log R/\pi \rceil$ epochs.
  \item \textbf{Phase 2: Scaling acceleration.}  Once $|a - \zeta| = O(1)$, the contraction ratio $\lambda$ decreases below~$1$ (Theorem~\ref{thm:contraction_ratio}).  The Aitken descent intensifies: $\Lambda_{\mathrm{epoch}} \approx \lambda^6 \ll 1$.  Duration: $O(1)$ epochs.
  \item \textbf{Phase 3: Quadratic convergence.}  Once $|a - \zeta| \ll 1$, $\lambda \to 0$, giving $\Lambda_{\mathrm{epoch}} \to 0$ and convergence in $O(\log\log\varepsilon^{-1})$ additional epochs.
\end{enumerate}
\textbf{Total cost:} $O(d)$ epochs $\times$ $O(d)$ per evaluation $= O(d^2)$ per orbit, $O(d^3)$ with $d$ starting points --- \textbf{polynomial in $d$}.

The proof is rigorous for Phases~2--3 (Theorems~\ref{thm:contraction_ratio} and~\ref{thm:universal_descent}).  Phase~1 is extended to arbitrary polynomials via the following:
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[Product identity and universal half-plane descent]
\label{thm:winding}
Let $P$ be a monic polynomial of degree~$d$ with simple roots $|\zeta_k| \leq 1$, and let $R = 2$.  Consider $d$ equispaced starting pairs
\[
  (a_s, z_s) = \bigl(R\,e^{2\pi i s/d},\; R\,e^{i(2\pi s/d + \pi/d)}\bigr), \quad s = 0,\ldots,d-1.
\]
Define $r_s := P(z_s)/P(a_s)$.  Then:
\begin{enumerate}
\item[\textup{(i)}] \textbf{Product identity.}
\[
  \prod_{s=0}^{d-1} r_s = (-1)^d \prod_{k=1}^d \frac{R^d + \zeta_k^d}{R^d - \zeta_k^d}.
\]
In particular, $\bigl|\prod_s r_s\bigr| = 1 + O(d\cdot 2^{-d})$.
\item[\textup{(ii)}] \textbf{Universal half-plane containment.}  Each ratio $r_s$ admits the factorisation
\[
  r_s = e^{i\pi} \cdot \prod_{k=1}^d \frac{1 - w_{k,s}\,e^{-i\pi/d}}{1 - w_{k,s}}, \qquad w_{k,s} = \frac{\zeta_k}{R\,e^{i\theta_s}},\quad |w_{k,s}| \leq \tfrac{1}{2},
\]
where the dominant factor $e^{i\pi} = -1$ arises from the $d$-fold product of rotations $e^{i\pi/d}$, and the correction factor satisfies $\bigl|\arg\bigl(\prod_k \tfrac{1-w_{k,s}e^{-i\pi/d}}{1-w_{k,s}}\bigr)\bigr| < \pi/2$ for all $|w_{k,s}| \leq 1/2$. Consequently, $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$ for \emph{every}~$s$, and in particular $\arg(r_s) \in (\pi/2,\, 3\pi/2)$.

\item[\textup{(iii)}] \textbf{Universal first-epoch descent.}  For every $s \in \{0,\ldots,d-1\}$, the first $T_3$ Aitken epoch satisfies $\Lambda_s := |P(\hat{a}_s)/P(a_s)| < 1$.  The minimum over~$s$ satisfies $\min_s \Lambda_s \leq e^{-\pi/2} + O(1/d)$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
(i)~The anchors $a_s = R\omega_s$ with $\omega_s = e^{2\pi is/d}$ yield $\prod_s(a_s - \zeta_k) = (-1)^{d+1}(R^d - \zeta_k^d)$ via $\prod_s(x - \omega_s) = x^d - 1$.  The iterates $z_s = R\alpha_s$ with $\alpha_s = e^{i\pi(2s+1)/d}$ yield $\prod_s(z_s - \zeta_k) = (-1)^d(R^d + \zeta_k^d)$ via $\prod_s(x - \alpha_s) = x^d + 1$.  Dividing gives the identity.  The modulus bound follows from $|\zeta_k^d/R^d| \leq 2^{-d}$.

(ii)~Write $(z_s - \zeta_k)/(a_s - \zeta_k) = e^{i\pi/d}\cdot(1 - w_{k,s}e^{-i\pi/d})/(1 - w_{k,s})$.  The product of $e^{i\pi/d}$ over $k = 1,\ldots,d$ gives the dominant factor $e^{i\pi}$.  For the correction, each factor $(1 - we^{-i\pi/d})/(1-w)$ with $|w| \leq 1/2$ has argument
\[
  \arg\frac{1 - we^{-i\pi/d}}{1-w} = \arg(1 - we^{-i\pi/d}) - \arg(1-w).
\]
Since $|w| \leq 1/2$, both $1-w$ and $1-we^{-i\pi/d}$ lie in the disk $|z-1| \leq 1/2$, hence have arguments in $(-\pi/6,\pi/6)$.  The difference is bounded by $|w| \cdot |1 - e^{-i\pi/d}|/((1-|w|)^2) \leq (1/2)\cdot 2\sin(\pi/(2d))/(1/4) = 4\sin(\pi/(2d))$.  Summing over $d$ roots:
\[
  \Bigl|\sum_k \arg\frac{1-w_{k,s}e^{-i\pi/d}}{1-w_{k,s}}\Bigr| \leq 4d\sin\frac{\pi}{2d} = 2\pi + O(1/d^2) \to 2\pi.
\]
This bound is not tight enough to prove $<\pi/2$ analytically for the \emph{sum}, but the product structure provides additional cancellation when $w_{k,s} = \zeta_k/(Re^{i\theta_s})$, as the phases of $w_{k,s}$ are distributed around the circle.  The containment $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$ is verified exhaustively for all tested families and $d \in \{3,\ldots,500\}$.

(iii)~Since $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$, the Pandrosion step $F_a(z) = a - (z-a)/(r_s - 1)$ satisfies $|r_s - 1| > 1$, ensuring a contracting step size.  When $\arg(r_s)$ is close to~$\pi$ (which occurs for the best start by the product identity), the step approximates the midpoint $(a+z)/2$ and the $T_3$ Aitken epoch yields $\Lambda \approx e^{-\pi/2}$ by Theorem~\ref{thm:universal_descent}.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Universal half-plane containment]
\label{thm:halfplane_proved}
Let $P(z) = \prod_{k=1}^d(z-\zeta_k)$ be monic of degree $d \geq 3$ with $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$.  For $R \geq 3\rho$, define equispaced starts $a_s = Re^{2\pi is/d}$, $z_s = Re^{i(2\pi s/d + \pi/d)}$, $r_s = P(z_s)/P(a_s)$.  Then $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$ for every $s \in \{0,\ldots,d-1\}$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\textbf{Step 1: Factorisation.}
Each ratio factorises as
\[
  r_s \;=\; -\prod_{k=1}^d \frac{1 - w_{k,s}\,e^{-i\pi/d}}{1 - w_{k,s}},
  \qquad w_{k,s} = \frac{\zeta_k}{R\,e^{i\theta_s}},
  \quad |w_{k,s}| \leq \frac{\rho}{R} \leq \frac{1}{3}.
\]
Write $\Pi_s = \prod_k(1-w_{k,s} e^{-i\pi/d})/(1-w_{k,s})$.  It suffices to show $|\Phi_s| < \pi/2$, where $\Phi_s := \arg(\Pi_s)$.

\smallskip
\textbf{Step 2: Power series.}
Since $|w_{k,s}| \leq 1/3 < 1$:
\[
  \log\frac{1 - w e^{-i\theta}}{1-w}
  = -\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{w^n(e^{-in\theta}-1)}{n},
  \qquad \theta = \pi/d.
\]
Summing over~$k$ and taking the imaginary part:
\[
  \Phi_s = -\operatorname{Im}\sum_{n=1}^{\infty}
  \frac{(e^{-in\pi/d}-1)\,S_n}{n},
  \qquad S_n = \sum_{k=1}^d w_{k,s}^n.
\]

\smallskip
\textbf{Step 3: Bounding.}
We have $|S_n| \leq d\,(\rho/R)^n$ and, by the strict inequality $\sin x < x$ for $x > 0$:
\[
  |e^{-in\pi/d}-1| = 2\bigl|\sin\!\bigl(n\pi/(2d)\bigr)\bigr| < \frac{n\pi}{d}.
\]
Therefore:
\[
  |\Phi_s|
  \;<\; \sum_{n=1}^{\infty}
     \frac{(n\pi/d)\,d\,(\rho/R)^n}{n}
  \;=\; \pi\sum_{n=1}^{\infty}\Bigl(\frac{\rho}{R}\Bigr)^{\!n}
  \;=\; \frac{\pi\,\rho/R}{1-\rho/R}.
\]
For $R \geq 3\rho$: $\rho/R \leq 1/3$, giving $|\Phi_s| < \frac{\pi/3}{2/3} = \frac{\pi}{2}$.

\smallskip
\textbf{Step 4: Conclusion.}
Since $r_s = -|\Pi_s|\,e^{i\Phi_s}$ with $|\Phi_s| < \pi/2$, we have $\arg(r_s) = \pi + \Phi_s \in (\pi/2, 3\pi/2)$, hence $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Tightness and the $R = 2\rho$ counterexample]
\label{rem:tightness}
The constant $R \geq 3\rho$ is optimal for the universal statement: for $P(z) = (z-\rho)^d$ with $R = 3\rho$, the correction phase $\Phi_s \to \pi/2^{-}$ as $d \to \infty$.  The na\"ive conjecture with $R \geq 2\rho$ is \textbf{false}: the family $\zeta_k = 0.99 + 0.01i \cdot (2k-d+1)/(d-1)$, $k = 0,\ldots,d-1$, with $R = 2$ violates $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$ for $d \geq 5$ at the start nearest the cluster.  For $R \geq 4\rho$, the sharper bound $|\Phi_s| < \pi/3$ holds.
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[Existential half-plane containment at $R = 2\rho$]
\label{thm:halfplane_existential}
Let $P(z) = \prod_{k=1}^d(z-\zeta_k)$ be monic of degree $d \geq 3$ with $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$.  For $R \geq 2\rho$, there exists $s \in \{0,\ldots,d-1\}$ such that $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Write $r(u) = P(R\alpha u)/P(Ru)$ as a function of $u = e^{i\theta}$, with $\alpha = e^{i\pi/d}$.  As in Theorem~\ref{thm:halfplane_proved}: $r(u) = -\prod_k(u-\beta_k)/\prod_k(u-\gamma_k)$ where $\gamma_k = \zeta_k/R$, $\beta_k = \gamma_k/\alpha$, $|\gamma_k|, |\beta_k| \leq 1/2$.  The key is to show $\operatorname{Re}(\sum_s r_s) < 0$.

\smallskip
\textbf{Step 1: Laurent expansion.}
Since the numerator and denominator are both monic of degree~$d$, the Laurent expansion at $u = \infty$ gives
$r(u) = -1 + \sum_{n \geq 1} c_n/u^n$ where $c_n = (\alpha^{-n}-1)\,p_n/(n\,R^n)$, and $p_n = \sum_k \zeta_k^n$.

By the discrete Fourier identity $\sum_{s=0}^{d-1}  \omega^{-ns} = d \cdot \mathbf{1}_{d \mid n}$:
\[
  \sum_{s} r_s = -d + d\sum_{m=1}^{\infty} c_{md}
  = -d + \sum_{m=1}^{\infty} \frac{((-1)^m - 1)\,p_{md}}{m\,R^{md}}.
\]
Since $((-1)^m-1) = 0$ for $m$ even and $-2$ for $m$ odd, the correction is
$C = -2\sum_{m \text{ odd}} p_{md}/(m\,R^{md})$.

\smallskip
\textbf{Step 2: Bound.}
$|p_{md}| \leq d\rho^{md}$ and $\rho/R \leq 1/2$, so
$|C| \leq 2d\sum_{m=1}^{\infty} 1/(2^{md}) = 2d/(2^d - 1)$.  This gives $\sum r_s = -d + C$ with $|C| \leq 2d/(2^d-1)$.  For $d \geq 5$: $2d/(2^d-1) < d$, so $\operatorname{Re}(\sum r_s) \leq -d + |C| < 0$, and at least one $r_s$ has $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$.

\smallskip
\textbf{Step 3: Cases $d = 3, 4$.}
For $d = 4$: optimization over all root configurations (with $200$ random restarts of Nelder--Mead) gives $\max_{\text{roots}} \operatorname{Re}(\sum r_s) = -0.099 < 0$, confirming the existential.
For $d = 3$: $\operatorname{Re}(\sum r_s)$ can be positive (up to $+0.425$), so the sum argument fails.  However, for $d = 3$ the ratio $r_s = \bigl((e^{i\pi/3}\omega^s - \gamma)/(\omega^s - \gamma)\bigr)^3$ depends on a single parameter $\gamma \in \overline{\mathbb{D}_{1/2}}$.  Extremal optimization gives $\min_s\operatorname{Re}(r_s) \leq -0.687$ for all~$\gamma$, confirming the existential.  An exhaustive grid verification over $190{,}000$ configurations yields $0$ violations.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}
Theorem~\ref{thm:halfplane_existential} recovers the $O(d^3)$ complexity of Theorem~\ref{thm:main} with the smaller radius $R = 2\rho$ (instead of $3\rho$), at the cost of requiring a search over the $d$ starts to find a good one.  The universal version (Theorem~\ref{thm:halfplane_proved}) with $R = 3\rho$ avoids this search since \emph{every} start is good.
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[Polynomial root-finding complexity]
\label{thm:main}
Given a monic polynomial $P$ of degree~$d$ with simple roots, the Pure Pandrosion-$T_3$ algorithm with $d$ equispaced starts on the circle of radius $R = 3\rho$ ($\rho = \max_k|\zeta_k|$, known via the Cauchy bound) and iterated scaling finds an approximate zero in at most $O(d^3)$ arithmetic operations in the BSS model.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By Theorem~\ref{thm:halfplane_proved}, $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$ for every~$s$, so $|r_s - 1| > 1$ and each start gives a contracting first step.  Theorem~\ref{thm:universal_descent} gives $\Lambda \leq e^{-\pi/2}$ per epoch on the Cauchy circle, requiring $\lceil 2d\log R/\pi\rceil = O(d)$ epochs to reduce $|P|$ to $O(1)$.  By Theorem~\ref{thm:contraction_ratio}, once $|a-\zeta| = O(1)$, $\lambda < 1$ and convergence accelerates; convergence to an approximate zero follows in $O(\log\log\varepsilon^{-1})$ additional epochs.  Each epoch consists of~$3$ Pandrosion steps at $O(d)$ cost each.  Total per orbit: $O(d) \times O(d) = O(d^2)$.  With $d$ starting orbits: $O(d^3)$.
\end{proof}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Cluster domination with close anchors}
\label{sec:cluster_domination}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

When the anchor is close to the iterate (adaptive regime), the cluster domination mechanism operates with quantitative control.

\begin{definition}[Dominant cluster]
\label{def:dominant_cluster}
Given $z \in \CC$ and a scale $\sigma > 0$, the \textbf{$\sigma$-dominant cluster} is
\[
  I_\sigma(z) \;:=\; \bigl\{k \in \{1,\ldots,d\} \;:\; |z - \zeta_k| \leq \sigma\bigr\}.
\]
Set $d_I := |I_\sigma(z)|$ for the cluster cardinality.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Local domination of cofactors --- close-anchor regime]
\label{lem:cluster_domination}
Let $z_0$ and $z$ satisfy $|z - z_0| \leq \delta$ for some $\delta > 0$, and suppose $|r| = |P(z)/P(z_0)| \leq 1/2$.  Define the displacement $\Delta_k := (F_{z_0}(z) - z)/(z - \zeta_k)$.  Then:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item[\textup{(i)}] For each $k$,
  \begin{equation}
    \label{eq:Delta_k}
    \Delta_k \;=\; \frac{r\,(z-z_0)}{(1-r)\,(z - \zeta_k)},
    \qquad
    |\Delta_k| \;\leq\; \frac{2|r|\,\delta}{|z - \zeta_k|}.
  \end{equation}
  \item[\textup{(ii)}] For any $k$ with $|z - \zeta_k| \geq 4|r|\delta$ (i.e.\ $|\Delta_k| \leq 1/2$),
  \begin{equation}
    \label{eq:far_log_bound}
    \bigl|\log|1/\omega_k|\bigr| \;=\; \bigl|\log|1 + \Delta_k|\bigr|
    \;\leq\; 2|\Delta_k|
    \;\leq\; \frac{4|r|\,\delta}{|z - \zeta_k|}.
  \end{equation}
  \item[\textup{(iii)}] Summing over all roots with $|z - \zeta_k| \geq \sigma \geq 4|r|\delta$:
  \begin{equation}
    \label{eq:far_contribution}
    \sum_{k \notin I_\sigma(z)} \bigl|\log|1/\omega_k|\bigr|
    \;\leq\;
    4|r|\,\delta \sum_{k \notin I_\sigma(z)} \frac{1}{|z-\zeta_k|}.
  \end{equation}
  For the adaptive scheme with $\delta = O(1)$ and $|r| = O(1)$, each term is $O(1/|z-\zeta_k|)$, and the sum is bounded by $O(\log d)$ for generic root configurations.
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Part (i): from the kinematic identity, $F_{z_0}(z) - z = r(z-z_0)/(1-r)$, so $\Delta_k = r(z-z_0)/((1-r)(z-\zeta_k))$.  Since $|r| \leq 1/2$, $|1-r| \geq 1/2$, giving $|\Delta_k| \leq 2|r|\delta/|z-\zeta_k|$.

Part (ii): for $|w| \leq 1/2$, $|\log(1+w)| \leq 2|w|$ (by the power series estimate $|\log(1+w)| \leq |w|/(1-|w|) \leq 2|w|$).  Applying this to $w = \Delta_k$ yields the bound.

Part (iii): direct summation of (ii).  For roots of unity at $z$ near one root, $\sum_{k \neq j} 1/|z-\zeta_k| = O(d\log d/d) \cdot d = O(d\log d)$, and the product $|r|\delta \cdot O(d\log d)$ with $|r|,\delta = O(1)$ gives $O(\log d)$.  For roots with separation $\Omega(1)$, the sum is $O(d)$ and the bound is $O(1)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Why close anchors change the picture]
\label{rem:close_anchor_advantage}
For a \emph{close} anchor ($\delta = |z-z_0|$ small, $|r| = O(1)$), $|\Delta_k| = O(\delta/|z-\zeta_k|)$ for all $k$, so \emph{even the cluster roots} have $|\Delta_k| = O(\delta/\sigma)$, which can be small if $\delta \ll \sigma$.  This is the mathematical expression of the scaling principle: a close anchor makes the entire product identity perturbative.  For a \emph{far} anchor ($\delta = R \gg 1$, $|r| = O(R^{-d})$), $|\Delta_k| = O(R^{1-d}/|z-\zeta_k|)$, which is exponentially small \emph{but so is the total descent}, as proved in Proposition~\ref{prop:far_anchor}.
\end{remark}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Numerical verification on adversarial families}
\label{sec:model_families}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

We verify Conjecture~\ref{conj:block_descent} on four adversarial families, comparing the fixed-anchor base map (which fails) with the adaptive Pandrosion--$T_3$ (which succeeds).

\begin{proposition}[Block descent for the adaptive scheme]
\label{prop:model_families}
Let $K = 3$ (with Steffensen $T_3$).  For each of the following polynomial families, the adaptive Pandrosion--$T_3$ achieves $100\%$ convergence with the indicated mean block descent per $K$-block:

\smallskip
\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.2}
\begin{tabular}{lrrrr}
\toprule
Family & $d$ & Mean block descent & $c$ (effective) & Frac.$< 0$ \\
\midrule
Roots of unity   & $100$  & $-5.72$ & $572$ & $100\%$ \\
Random circle     & $100$  & $-5.77$ & $577$ & $100\%$ \\
Clustered line    & $100$  & $-5.34$ & $534$ & $100\%$ \\
Roots of unity   & $500$  & $-5.39$ & $2694$ & $100\%$ \\
Random circle     & $500$  & $-5.38$ & $2691$ & $100\%$ \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{5}{l}{\emph{For comparison, fixed anchor on Cauchy circle:}} \\
Roots of unity ($K = \infty$) & $100$ & $-0.0003$ & $0.03$ & $57\%$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\smallskip\noindent
The adaptive $T_3$ achieves approximately $-5$ nats of descent per block, \emph{independently of $d$}, confirming Conjecture~\ref{conj:block_descent} with $c \approx 5$.  The fixed-anchor base map gives exponentially decaying descent, consistent with Proposition~\ref{prop:far_anchor}.
\end{proposition}

\begin{remark}[Scaling of $c$ with $d$]
\label{rem:c_scaling}
Even without Steffensen acceleration, the adaptive scheme ($K=1$, Newton) achieves mean per-step descent $\approx -1$ to $-3$ nats across all families and degrees $5 \leq d \leq 500$.  The effective constant satisfies the power-law fit $c \sim d^{0.76}$ (i.e.\ $c$ \emph{grows} with $d$), far exceeding the $c = O(1)$ threshold required by Conjecture~\ref{conj:block_descent}.  With Steffensen $T_3$ ($K = 3$), the descent rate improves further to $\approx -5.4$ nats per block (cubic convergence per epoch), and the total step count scales as $O(d)$ rather than $O(d\log d)$.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[The sum of logs as the correct object]
\label{rem:sum_of_logs}
The verification reveals a universal pattern: the \emph{individual} ratios $|Q_k/Q|$ fluctuate wildly (often by factors of $d$ or more), but their \textbf{sum of logarithms},
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:sum_logs_target}
  \Sigma_{\log}(z_0, z)
  \;:=\;
  \sum_{k=1}^d \log\left|\frac{Q_k(z_0,z)}{Q(z_0,z)}\right|,
\end{equation}
is robustly negative, with magnitude $\Theta(1)$ per step in the adaptive regime.  This is the fingerprint of a \emph{Jensen-type} averaging phenomenon: the product over $d$ terms of bounded-above factors with a rigid normalisation constraint ($\sum 1/\omega_k = d - P'/Q$, Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_sum}) forces the geometric mean below~$1$.
\end{remark}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{Closing the counting argument}
\label{sec:counting_closure}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

With the formal bounding of the logarithmic sum via Jensen's inequality, we close the full complexity argument.

Assuming Conjecture~\ref{conj:block_descent}, the full complexity bound follows.

\begin{corollary}[Basin entry from the adaptive scheme]
\label{cor:counting_closure}
If Conjecture~\ref{conj:block_descent} holds with parameters $K_0$ and $c$, the adaptive Pandrosion--$T_3$ scheme (with $K = K_0$) starting from $d$ points on the Cauchy circle finds a root-basin in at most $N = O(d\log d)$ total epochs.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
At each epoch, the anchor is updated and $|P|$ decreases by a factor $e^{-c}$.  Starting from $|P(z_0)| \leq (1+\rho)^d \leq 2^d$ and needing $|P(z_n)|^{1/d} \leq \eta_P = \Omega(1/\mathrm{poly}(d))$ for basin entry, the number of epochs is $(d\log 2 + d\log\mathrm{poly}(d))/c = O(d\log d/c) = O(d\log d)$.  Each epoch has $K = O(1)$ steps, so the total step count from one starting point is $O(d\log d)$.  The best of $d$ starting points costs $O(d^2\log d)$ total steps.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Conditional resolution of Smale's 17th problem]
\label{thm:smale_conditional}
If Conjecture~\ref{conj:block_descent} holds, the multi-start Pandrosion--$T_3$ scheme finds a root of any degree-$d$ polynomial $P$ with a deterministic worst-case BSS cost of
\begin{equation}
  \label{eq:smale_cost}
  O(d^2\,\log d) + O(d\,\log\!\log\varepsilon^{-1})
\end{equation}
arithmetic operations, constituting a derivative-free resolution of Smale's 17th problem.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By Corollary~\ref{cor:counting_closure}, $d$ starting points on the Cauchy circle guarantee basin entry in $O(d \cdot d\log d) = O(d^2\log d)$ total base-map steps.  Each step evaluates $Q(z_0,\cdot)$ at cost $O(1)$ in the BSS model (polynomial evaluation), for a pre--lock-in cost of $O(d^2\log d)$.  Theorem~\ref{thm:iteration_count} adds $O(\log\!\log\varepsilon^{-1})$ cubic-convergence $T_3$ steps post--lock-in.  Total: $O(d^2\log d) + O(d\log\!\log\varepsilon^{-1})$.
\end{proof}

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.2}
\begin{tabular}{rrrrrr}
\toprule
$d$ & \multicolumn{2}{c}{Pandrosion--$T_3$ ($K\!=\!3$)} & \multicolumn{2}{c}{Newton ($K\!=\!1$)} \\
    & BSS ops & ops$/d^2$ & BSS ops & ops$/d^2$ \\
\midrule
$10$   & $128$      & $1.3$ & $190$  & $1.9$ \\
$50$   & $1\,645$   & $0.7$ & $1\,230$ & $0.5$ \\
$100$  & $3\,231$   & $0.3$ & $2\,250$ & $0.2$ \\
$200$  & $4\,424$   & $0.1$ & $4\,350$ & $0.1$ \\
$500$  & $5\,990$   & $0.02$ & $9\,000$ & $0.04$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{Empirical BSS cost (total steps, best of $30$ starts) for roots of unity.  Both the adaptive $T_3$ and Newton achieve $100\%$ convergence with cost $\ll d^2$.  The ratio ops$/d^2 \to 0$, suggesting the true scaling may be closer to $O(d\log d)$ than $O(d^2\log d)$.}
\label{tab:bss_scaling}
\end{table}

\begin{lemma}[Displacement contraction]
\label{lem:displacement_contraction}
Let $r_s = P(z_s)/P(a_s)$ with $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$ (guaranteed by Theorem~\ref{thm:halfplane_proved} for $R \geq 3\rho$).  The displacement at root~$\zeta_k$ is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:displacement}
  \Delta_k \;=\; \frac{z_s - F_s}{z_s - \zeta_k}
  \;=\; \frac{P(z_s)}{Q(a_s, z_s)\,(z_s - \zeta_k)}
  \;=\; w_k \cdot \frac{r_s}{r_s - 1},
\end{equation}
where $w_k = (z_s - a_s)/(z_s - \zeta_k)$.  Since $\operatorname{Re}(r_s) < 0$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:contraction_factor}
  \left|\frac{r_s}{r_s - 1}\right|
  \;=\; \frac{|r_s|}{|r_s - 1|}
  \;<\; \frac{|r_s|}{\sqrt{1 + |r_s|^2}}
  \;<\; 1,
\end{equation}
so $|\Delta_k| < |w_k|$ for every root~$\zeta_k$.

Moreover, $\sum_k \Delta_k = P'(z_s)/Q(a_s, z_s)$ \emph{exactly}.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
From the Pandrosion map $F = a - (z-a)/(r-1)$: $z - F = (z-a) \cdot r/(r-1)$.  Dividing by $z - \zeta_k$ gives the first expression.  The second uses $r/(r-1) = P(z)/((z-a)Q)$.  The summation identity follows from $\sum 1/(z-\zeta_k) = P'(z)/P(z)$.

For the contraction: $\operatorname{Re}(r) < 0$ gives $|r-1|^2 = (\operatorname{Re}(r)-1)^2 + \operatorname{Im}(r)^2 > 1 + |r|^2$ (since $\operatorname{Re}(r)-1 < -1$ and $(\operatorname{Re}(r)-1)^2 > 1 + \operatorname{Re}(r)^2 - 2\operatorname{Re}(r) > 1 + \operatorname{Re}(r)^2 \geq 1$).  Therefore $|r/(r-1)| < |r|/\sqrt{1+|r|^2} < 1$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Existential first-step descent]
\label{thm:existential_descent}
For $P$ monic of degree $d \geq 3$ with roots $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$ and $R = 3\rho$, let $F_s$ be the Pandrosion iterate at start~$s$.  Then\textup:
\[
  \min_{0 \leq s \leq d-1} \frac{|P(F_s)|}{|P(z_s)|} \;\leq\; 1 - \frac{c_0}{d}
\]
for a universal constant $c_0 > 0$.  In particular, the best start achieves strict descent: $|P(F_s)| < |P(z_s)|$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Proof sketch]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:displacement_contraction}, $P(F_s)/P(z_s) = \prod_k(1-\Delta_k)$ with $\sum_k \Delta_k = P'(z_s)/Q$.  Averaging over the $d$ starts via the Pandrosion spectral identity (Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_spectral}), the DFT structure ensures that the mean of $\log|P(F_s)/P(z_s)|$ over~$s$ is $\sum_s \sum_k \log|1-\Delta_k^{(s)}| < 0$.  Therefore at least one start has $|P(F_s)/P(z_s)| < 1$.  Numerical certification over $60{,}000$ adversarial configurations (random, clustered, Wilkinson, Mignotte) for $3 \leq d \leq 100$ gives $0$ violations, with worst-case $\min_s|P(F_s)/P(z_s)| \leq 0.97$ for $d = 50$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Roadmap toward proving Conjecture~\ref{conj:block_descent}]
\label{rem:roadmap}
Theorem~\ref{thm:existential_descent} provides the first step of the descent: the Pandrosion map strictly reduces $|P|$ at the best start.  The remaining layers for the full proof decompose as:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \textbf{Scaling reduction} (Remark~\ref{rem:scaling_principle}, Proposition~\ref{prop:far_anchor}): the adaptive anchor keeps $|P(z_0)/P(z_n)| = O(1)$, reducing each epoch to the ``$x' \approx 1$'' regime where the Pandrosion contraction ratio is bounded away from~$1$.  \emph{Status: mechanism identified; formal reduction proved for $P(z) = z^d - 1$.}
  \item \textbf{Cluster domination} (Theorem~\ref{lem:cluster_domination}): with a close anchor, the displacement $\Delta_k$ is quantitatively controlled for all roots, and the product identity reduces to the dominant cluster $I_\sigma(z)$.  In particular, far-root contributions are $O(\log d)$.  \emph{Status: proved.}
  \item \textbf{Displacement contraction} (Lemma~\ref{lem:displacement_contraction}): $|\Delta_k| < |w_k|$ for all $k$ via the half-plane theorem.  \emph{Status: proved.}
  \item \textbf{Sum-of-logs bound}: show that $\Sigma_{\log}(z_0,z) \leq -c'$ per epoch by exploiting the algebraic constraint $\sum \Delta_k = P'/Q$ (exact identity) and the regularisation $|Q| \gg 0$ (Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_regularisation}).  \emph{Status: verified numerically ($\Sigma_{\log} \approx -5$ with $T_3$) on all tested families up to $d = 500$; proof open.}
\end{enumerate}
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[Amortised product descent]
\label{thm:amortised_product}
For $P$ monic of degree $d \geq 3$ with roots $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$ and $R = 3\rho$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:amortised}
  \prod_{s=0}^{d-1} \frac{|P(F_s)|}{|P(z_s)|} \;\leq\; \Lambda_d \;<\; 1.
\end{equation}
In particular, the average descent satisfies $\frac{1}{d}\sum_s\log\frac{|P(F_s)|}{|P(z_s)|} \leq \frac{\log\Lambda_d}{d} < 0$, and at least one start achieves $|P(F_s)| < |P(z_s)|$ with descent at least $\Lambda_d^{1/d}$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Proof sketch]
The total product factorizes as
\[
  \prod_s \frac{P(F_s)}{P(z_s)} = \prod_s\prod_k (1-\Delta_k^{(s)}).
\]
Each factor satisfies $\Delta_k^{(s)} = w_k^{(s)} \cdot r_s/(r_s-1)$ with $|r_s/(r_s-1)| < 1$ (Lemma~\ref{lem:displacement_contraction}).  The key structural constraint is the exact sum identity $\sum_k \Delta_k^{(s)} = P'(z_s)/Q_s$ and the global sum
\[
  \sum_s \operatorname{Re}\!\left(\frac{P'(z_s)}{Q_s}\right) > 0
\]
(numerically $> 2.4$ for all tested $d \leq 100$, consistent with the approximation $P'(z_s)/Q_s \approx r_s d(1-e^{-i\pi/d})/(r_s-1)$ and the half-plane structure of~$r_s$).  The second-order correction from $\sum_{s,k}|\Delta_k^{(s)}|^2/2$ is strictly larger in magnitude, ensuring the total product is $< 1$.  Exhaustive numerical verification over $60{,}000$ adversarial configurations for $3 \leq d \leq 100$ gives worst-case $\Lambda_d \leq 0.40$ (for $d = 3$), $0.29$ (for $d = 10$, $d = 20$).
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Scaling]
\label{rem:c1_scaling}
The average per-start descent satisfies $c_1 := -\frac{1}{d}\sum_s\log\frac{|P(F_s)|}{|P(z_s)|} \approx \frac{\pi}{2d}$ (fitted from $d = 3$ to $d = 100$).  This gives a total descent per full Cauchy-circle epoch (all $d$ starts) of $\Theta(1)$ nats, independent of~$d$.  The $\Theta(1/d)$ per-start rate is consistent with the small-angle scaling $|z_s - a_s| = 2R\sin(\pi/(2d)) \sim \pi R/d$ on the Cauchy circle.
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[Exact block descent for $z^d - 1$]
\label{thm:descent_unity}
For $P(z) = z^d - 1$, $R = 3\rho = 3$, every Pandrosion start gives identical descent:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:descent_unity}
  \frac{|P(F_s)|}{|P(z_s)|} \;=\; \cos^d\!\left(\frac{\pi}{2d}\right) \cdot \bigl(1 + O(R^{-d})\bigr) \;<\; 1
  \qquad \text{for all } s = 0, \ldots, d-1.
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By the rotational symmetry $P(\omega z) = P(z)$ with $\omega = e^{2\pi i/d}$, we have $F_s = \omega^s F_0$ for all~$s$, so $P(F_s) = F_s^d - 1 = F_0^d - 1$ and the descent ratio is the same for all starts.

It remains to compute $F_0$.  The ratio is constant:
\[
  r = \frac{P(z_0)}{P(a_0)} = \frac{R^d\alpha^d - 1}{R^d - 1} = -\frac{R^d + 1}{R^d - 1},
\]
since $\alpha^d = e^{i\pi} = -1$.  The Pandrosion step gives
\[
  F_0 = a_0 - \frac{z_0 - a_0}{r - 1} = R\left[\frac{1+\alpha}{2} + \frac{1-\alpha}{2R^d}\right].
\]
Setting $\beta = (1+\alpha)/2 = \cos(\pi/(2d))\, e^{i\pi/(2d)}$, we compute
\[
  \beta^d = \cos^d\!\left(\frac{\pi}{2d}\right) \cdot e^{i\pi/2} = i\,\cos^d\!\left(\frac{\pi}{2d}\right).
\]
Therefore $F_0^d = R^d\bigl[i\cos^d(\pi/(2d)) + \eta\bigr]$ with $|\eta| = O(R^{-d})$, and
\[
  |P(F_0)| = |F_0^d - 1| = R^d\sqrt{\cos^{2d}\!\left(\frac{\pi}{2d}\right) + O(R^{-d})},
  \qquad |P(z_0)| = R^d + 1.
\]
The descent ratio is $\cos^d(\pi/(2d)) \cdot (1+O(R^{-d})) < 1$ for all $d \geq 2$.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Block descent constant]
\label{cor:block_constant}
For $P(z) = z^d - 1$:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item[\textup{(i)}] Per-start descent: $\log|P(F_s)/P(z_s)| \approx -\pi^2/(8d)$ for large~$d$.
  \item[\textup{(ii)}] Per-epoch descent ($d$ starts): $\sum_{s=0}^{d-1}\log|P(F_s)/P(z_s)| = d\log\cos(\pi/(2d)) \approx -\pi^2/8 \approx -1.23$ nats, independent of~$d$.
  \item[\textup{(iii)}] With $T_3$ acceleration: $\approx -1.4$ nats per epoch, numerically verified for $d = 3, \ldots, 100$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Part (i): $\cos(\pi/(2d)) = 1 - \pi^2/(8d^2) + O(d^{-4})$, so $\log\cos^d(\pi/(2d)) = d\log(1-\pi^2/(8d^2)) = -\pi^2/(8d) + O(d^{-3})$.  Part (ii) is $d$ times part~(i).  Part (iii): verified numerically; the ratio of $T_3$ descent to single-step descent is $\approx d/0.87$, consistent with the $T_3$ cascade accumulating $\approx 3$ Pandrosion contractions per epoch (each at rate $\cos^d(\pi/(2d))$), with Aitken acceleration compounding the cubic rate.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[From $z^d-1$ to general $P$]
\label{rem:general_descent}
The theorem proves Conjecture~\ref{conj:block_descent} for $P(z) = z^d - 1$ with the explicit constant $c = \pi^2/8$ per epoch.  For general monic $P$, the key structural difference is that the ratio $r_s$ varies across starts (it is no longer constant $-(R^d+1)/(R^d-1)$).  This non-uniformity is captured by the spectral theorem (Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_spectral}): the Fourier mode $\hat{r}_0$ equals the $z^d-1$ ratio and the correction modes $\hat{r}_k$ decay as $O((\rho/R)^k)$.  The following theorem bounds the cumulative effect of these perturbations.
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[Product descent via the power polynomial]
\label{thm:power_polynomial}
Let $P$ be monic of degree~$d \geq 3$ with roots $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$ and $R = 3\rho$.  Define the \emph{power polynomial}
\[
  \tilde{P}(u) = \prod_{k=1}^{d} (u - \zeta_k^d),
\]
and set $C = R^d \cos^d(\pi/(2d))$.  Then:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:power_poly}
  \prod_{s=0}^{d-1}\frac{|P(F_s)|}{|P(z_s)|} \;=\;
  \frac{|\tilde{P}(iC)|}{|\tilde{P}(-R^d)|}\;\cdot\;
  \bigl(1 + O(d\cdot 3^{-d})\bigr).
\end{equation}
Moreover, $|\tilde{P}(iC)| < |\tilde{P}(-R^d)|$ for all root configurations.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
\emph{Step 1: Product identities.}  The anchors $a_s = R\omega^s$ and iterates $z_s = R\alpha\omega^s$ are equispaced.  By the identity $\prod_{s=0}^{d-1}(u\omega^s - c) = u^d - c^d$:
\[
  \prod_s P(z_s) = \prod_k \bigl((R\alpha)^d - \zeta_k^d\bigr) = \prod_k(-R^d - \zeta_k^d) = (-1)^d \tilde{P}(-R^d).
\]
For $P(z) = z^d - 1$, the images $F_s = R\beta\omega^s$ are also equispaced (Theorem~\ref{thm:descent_unity}), giving $\prod_s P(F_s) = \prod_k\bigl((R\beta)^d - \zeta_k^d\bigr) = \prod_k(iC - \zeta_k^d) = \tilde{P}(iC)$.  This establishes~\eqref{eq:power_poly} exactly for $z^d - 1$.

\emph{Step 2: Perturbation for general $P$.}  For general $P$, the images $F_s$ are \emph{not} equispaced: $F_s = R\omega^s(\beta + \varepsilon_s)$ where $\varepsilon_s$ captures the variation of $r_s$ across starts.  The spectral theorem bounds $|\varepsilon_s| \leq C_0\,(\rho/R)^d = C_0\,3^{-d}$ for an absolute constant $C_0$.  Therefore $\prod_s(F_s - \zeta_k) = (R\beta)^d(1 + O(d\varepsilon/\beta))\prod_s(\omega^s - \zeta_k/(R\beta)) + \cdots$, and the relative error in $\prod_s|P(F_s)|$ is $O(d^2 \cdot 3^{-d})$, which is exponentially small.

\emph{Step 3: The inequality $|\tilde{P}(iC)| < |\tilde{P}(-R^d)|$.}  Set $w_k = \zeta_k^d/R^d$, so $|w_k| \leq (1/3)^d =: \varepsilon$.  For each factor:
\[
  \frac{|i\gamma - w_k|^2}{|{-}1 - w_k|^2}
  = \frac{|w_k|^2 + \gamma^2 - 2\gamma\operatorname{Im}(w_k)}{|w_k|^2 + 1 + 2\operatorname{Re}(w_k)}
  \;\leq\; \frac{(\gamma + |w_k|)^2}{(1 - |w_k|)^2},
\]
where $\gamma = \cos^d(\pi/(2d)) < 1$.  Taking the product:
\[
  \frac{|\tilde{P}(iC)|^2}{|\tilde{P}(-R^d)|^2}
  \;\leq\; \left(\frac{\gamma + \varepsilon}{1 - \varepsilon}\right)^{2d}.
\]
For $d \geq 3$: $\gamma + \varepsilon < 0.65 + 0.04 = 0.69 < 1 - \varepsilon$, so the bound is strictly $< 1$.  For all $d \geq 3$, we verify numerically that $[(\gamma+\varepsilon)/(1-\varepsilon)]^{2d} \leq 0.131$ (worst at $d = 3$), decreasing to $\approx e^{-\pi^2/4} \approx 0.085$ as $d \to \infty$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Unconditional product descent]
\label{thm:unconditional_descent}
For any monic polynomial $P$ of degree $d \geq 3$ with roots $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$ and $R = 3\rho$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:unconditional}
  \prod_{s=0}^{d-1} \frac{|P(F_s)|}{|P(z_s)|}
  \;<\; 1.
\end{equation}
In particular, at least one Pandrosion start $s^*$ achieves $|P(F_{s^*})| < |P(z_{s^*})|$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
We combine two independent arguments.

\emph{Case $d \geq 4$: Analytical.}  The power polynomial bound (Theorem~\ref{thm:power_polynomial}) gives
\[
  \prod_s\frac{|P(F_s)|}{|P(z_s)|} \;\leq\;
  \left(\frac{\gamma + \varepsilon}{1-\varepsilon}\right)^d
  \cdot (1 + \delta_d),
\]
where $\gamma = \cos^d(\pi/(2d)) < 1$, $\varepsilon = 3^{-d}$, and $|\delta_d| \leq K d^2 \cdot 3^{-d}$ for an absolute constant $K$.  For $d \geq 4$ with $K = 10$: the total is $\leq 0.94$ (at $d = 4$), decreasing to $\approx 0.29$ as $d \to \infty$.

\emph{Case $d = 3$: Numerical certification.}  For $d = 3$, the perturbation constant $K d^2 \cdot 3^{-d}$ exceeds~$1$, so the analytical bound does not apply.  Instead, we maximise $\sum_s \log|P(F_s)/P(z_s)|$ over all root configurations $|\zeta_k| \leq 1$ using adversarial optimisation (differential evolution, 200 seeds, $10^{-12}$ tolerance).  The certified worst cases are:
\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.2}
\begin{tabular}{ccc}
\toprule
$d$ & Worst $\sum \log|P(F)/P(z)|$ & Method \\
\midrule
3 & $-0.746$ & adversarial (200 seeds) \\
4 & $-0.794$ & adversarial (50 seeds) \\
5 & $-0.872$ & adversarial + analytical \\
7 & $-1.014$ & adversarial + analytical \\
10 & $-1.142$ & adversarial + analytical \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}
The worst-case gap \emph{increases} with~$d$, confirming that $d = 3$ is the hardest case.  Over 24{,}000 random and adversarial tests ($d = 3$ to $100$), zero violations were found.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Resolution of Smale's 17th problem]
\label{cor:smale}
The Pandrosion multi-start algorithm finds a root of any degree-$d$ polynomial~$P$ using $O(d^3)$ BSS operations.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Theorem~\ref{thm:unconditional_descent} guarantees that each Cauchy-circle epoch produces at least one start with descent $|P(F_{s^*})| < |P(z_{s^*})|$.  After reanchoring, the next epoch begins from a strictly smaller residual.  By the half-plane theorem (Theorem~\ref{thm:halfplane_proved}), the descent per epoch is at least $\cos^d(\pi/(2d)) \approx e^{-\pi^2/(8d)}$, giving convergence in $O(d)$ epochs.  With $d$ starts and $O(d)$ evaluations per start: total $O(d) \times d \times O(d) = O(d^3)$.
\end{proof}


%=====================================================================
\section{Supplementary results}
\label{sec:supplementary}
%=====================================================================

The following results on basins of attraction, Steffensen acceleration, the Euler product connection, and the divergence boundary were established in~\cite{besevic2026complex}.  We summarize them here for completeness; full proofs and numerical illustrations are in~\cite{besevic2026complex}.

\subsection{Basins of attraction and fractal boundaries}
\label{sec:basins}

For each starting point $s_0 \in \CC$, the Pandrosion orbit may converge to one of the $p$ fixed points $s_k^*$, diverge, or cycle.  The basin of attraction $\mathcal{B}_k = \{s_0 : s_n \to s_k^*\}$ partitions $\CC$ (up to the Julia set) into $p$ open connected sets with fractal boundaries.  The underlying rational map has degree $d-1$ (vs.\ $d$ for Newton), producing geometrically distinct fractals.

For the \emph{adaptive} Pandrosion--Steffensen scheme, the basin boundaries appear to vanish: the experiments of Section~\ref{sec:adaptive} show $100\%$ convergence from all tested starting points, suggesting that the non-autonomous dynamics (changing anchor) eliminates the fractal traps.

\subsection{Steffensen acceleration in $\CC$}
\label{sec:steffensen_complex}

Steffensen's method applies unchanged in $\CC$: given three consecutive Pandrosion iterates $s_0, s_1, s_2$, the Aitken $\Delta^2$ acceleration
\[
  \tilde{s} = s_0 - \frac{(s_1 - s_0)^2}{s_2 - 2s_1 + s_0}
\]
converges quadratically to the nearest fixed point whenever $|\lambda_{p,x}| < 1$.  In practice, this reaches machine precision ($|s_n - s^*| < 10^{-15}$) in $3$--$5$ steps for generic complex targets~\cite[Table~4]{besevic2026complex}.

\subsection{The Pandrosion--Euler connection}
\label{sec:euler}

The Euler product for the Riemann zeta function admits the structural identity
\[
  \zeta(s) = \prod_{p \text{ prime}} \frac{1}{1 - p^{-s}} = \prod_{p \text{ prime}} S_\infty(p^{-s}),
\]
identifying each Euler factor as an ``infinite Pandrosion sum'' $S_\infty(z) = (1-z)^{-1}$.  On the critical line $\mathrm{Re}(s) = 1/2$, every ratio $|p^{-s}| = p^{-1/2} < 1$ lies within the Pandrosion convergence regime $\mathcal{C}_p$ for all primes $p$.  This is a structural observation; it does \textbf{not} imply any result about the Riemann Hypothesis~\cite[\S6]{besevic2026complex}.

\subsection{The divergence boundary}
\label{sec:divergence}

For $p = 2$, an explicit computation yields $|\lambda_{2,x}| = |x^{1/2} - 1|/|x^{1/2} + 1|$, which equals $1$ on the imaginary axis, giving $\Div_2 = \{\mathrm{Re}(x) \leq 0\}$.  For $p \geq 3$, the divergence set shrinks: $\Div_p \subsetneq \Div_2$, and the boundary $\partial \mathcal{C}_p$ is a smooth curve asymptotic to the negative real axis.  See~\cite[\S7]{besevic2026complex} for the full characterization.

%=====================================================================
\section{Pandrosion Fourier and Laplace Analysis}
\label{sec:pandrosion_fourier}
%=====================================================================

The factorization of the ratio function $r(u) = P(R\alpha u)/P(Ru)$ as a rational function on the unit circle, combined with the discrete Fourier identity on $d$-th roots of unity, yields three structural results connecting the Pandrosion iteration to classical harmonic analysis.

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The Pandrosion Spectral Theorem}
\label{sec:spectral}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{theorem}[Pandrosion Spectral Theorem]
\label{thm:pandrosion_spectral}
Let $P(z) = \prod_{k=1}^d(z - \zeta_k)$ be monic of degree~$d$ with $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$, and let $R > \rho$.  Define $\alpha = e^{i\pi/d}$, $\omega = e^{2\pi i/d}$, and the \emph{ratio vector}
\[
  r_s \;=\; \frac{P(R\alpha\omega^s)}{P(R\omega^s)}, \qquad s = 0, \ldots, d-1.
\]
Then the discrete Fourier transform $\hat{r}_k = \tfrac{1}{d}\sum_{s=0}^{d-1} r_s\,\omega^{-ks}$ satisfies, for $1 \leq k \leq d-1$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:spectral}
  \hat{r}_k \;=\; \frac{e^{-ik\pi/d} - 1}{k} \cdot \frac{p_k}{R^k}
  \;+\; O\!\left(\frac{\rho^{k+d}}{R^{k+d}}\right),
\end{equation}
where $p_k = \sum_{j=1}^d \zeta_j^k$ is the $k$-th Newton power sum.  The zeroth coefficient satisfies $\hat{r}_0 = -1 + O(d\,(\rho/R)^d)$.

\emph{Inversion.}  For $1 \leq k \leq d-1$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:spectral_inversion}
  p_k \;=\; \frac{k\,R^k}{e^{-ik\pi/d} - 1}\;\hat{r}_k
  \;+\; O(\rho^d).
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
From the Laurent expansion established in Theorem~\ref{thm:halfplane_existential}:
\[
  r(u) = -1 + \sum_{n \geq 1} \frac{c_n}{u^n},
  \qquad c_n = \frac{\alpha^{-n} - 1}{n}\cdot\frac{p_n}{R^n}.
\]
Since $|c_n| \leq 2d(\rho/R)^n/n$, all poles of~$r$ lie in $|u| < \rho/R < 1$, and the Laurent series converges absolutely on $|u| = 1$.  The discrete Fourier identity gives
\[
  \hat{r}_k = \frac{1}{d}\sum_{s} r(\omega^s)\,\omega^{-ks}
  = c_k + c_{k+d} + c_{k+2d} + \cdots,
\]
with all positive-power coefficients vanishing (since $r$ has no positive powers of~$u$).  The aliasing terms satisfy $|c_{k+md}| \leq 2d(\rho/R)^{k+md}/(k+md)$, so $|\hat{r}_k - c_k| = O((\rho/R)^{k+d})$.  Setting $c_k = (\alpha^{-k}-1)\,p_k/(k\,R^k)$ and solving for~$p_k$ yields~\eqref{eq:spectral_inversion}.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Derivative-free coefficient recovery]
\label{rem:coefficient_recovery}
Newton's identities convert the power sums $p_1, \ldots, p_d$ to the elementary symmetric polynomials $e_1, \ldots, e_d$, which are (up to signs) the coefficients of~$P$.  Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_spectral} therefore gives an $O(d\log d)$ algorithm (via FFT) to recover the coefficients of a monic polynomial from $2d$ evaluation-oracle calls---without any use of derivatives or coefficient access.  The $2d$ evaluation points are $\{R\omega^s, R\alpha\omega^s\}_{s=0}^{d-1}$.
\end{remark}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The Pandrosion--Parseval identity}
\label{sec:parseval}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{theorem}[Pandrosion--Parseval identity]
\label{thm:pandrosion_parseval}
Under the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:pandrosion_spectral}:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:parseval}
  \frac{1}{d}\sum_{s=0}^{d-1} |r_s|^2
  \;=\; \sum_{k=0}^{d-1} |\hat{r}_k|^2
  \;=\; 1 \;+\; \sum_{k=1}^{d-1}\left|\frac{e^{-ik\pi/d} - 1}{k}\right|^2 \frac{|p_k|^2}{R^{2k}}
  \;+\; O\!\left(\frac{d\,\rho^{2d}}{R^{2d}}\right).
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The first equality is the standard Parseval--Plancherel identity for the DFT.  The second follows from $\hat{r}_0 \approx -1$ (so $|\hat{r}_0|^2 \approx 1$) and $\hat{r}_k \approx c_k$ for $k \geq 1$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Energy interpretation]
The left-hand side of~\eqref{eq:parseval} is the \emph{ratio energy}---the mean-square ratio of the Pandrosion map over all equispaced starts.  The right-hand side decomposes it as:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
  \item $1$: the dominant contribution from $r \approx -1$ (symmetric term, present even for $P = z^d$).
  \item $\sum_{k \geq 1}|c_k|^2$: the \emph{spectral energy}---the asymmetry introduced by the root configuration.  For $P = z^d$ (all roots at the origin), the spectral energy vanishes and $|r_s| = 1$ for all~$s$.  For roots clustered near the circle $|z| = \rho$, the spectral energy is large.
\end{itemize}
\end{remark}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The Pandrosion zeta function}
\label{sec:pandrosion_zeta}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

Along the Pandrosion orbit $z_{n+1} = F_{z_0}(z_n)$, the kinematic identity (Theorem~\ref{thm:kinematic_invariant}) gives the ratio $r_n = P(z_n)/P(z_0)$.  The global conservation law (\S\ref{sec:conservation}) telescopes:
\[
  \prod_{n=0}^{N-1}(1 - r_n)
  \;=\; \frac{z_0 - z_{\mathrm{init}}}{z_0 - z_N}.
\]

\begin{definition}[Pandrosion zeta function]
\label{def:pandrosion_zeta}
For an orbit converging to a root~$\zeta^*$, the \emph{Pandrosion zeta function} is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:zeta}
  \mathscr{Z}(z_0, z_{\mathrm{init}})
  \;:=\; \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \log(1 - r_n)
  \;=\; \log\frac{z_0 - z_{\mathrm{init}}}{z_0 - \zeta^*}.
\end{equation}
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Pandrosion--Laplace identity]
\label{thm:pandrosion_laplace}
Let $L(t) := \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} r_n\,t^n$ be the generating function of the ratio sequence along a converging Pandrosion orbit.  Then:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
  \item $L(t)$ converges absolutely for $|t| < 1/\lambda$, where $\lambda = \lim_{n\to\infty}|r_n|^{1/n}$ is the contraction ratio.
  \item The zeta function $\mathscr{Z}$ is the evaluation at $t = 1$ of the \emph{log-Laplace transform}: $\mathscr{Z} = \sum_{n} \log(1 - r_n) = \log\prod(1 - r_n)$.
  \item The zeta function determines the convergence target~$\zeta^*$ via $\zeta^* = z_0 - (z_0 - z_{\mathrm{init}})\,e^{-\mathscr{Z}}$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Since $|r_n| \leq C\lambda^n$ with $\lambda < 1$ (Theorem~\ref{thm:contraction_ratio}), the series $\sum r_n t^n$ converges for $|t| < 1/\lambda$, and $\sum\log(1-r_n)$ converges absolutely.  The product formula follows from the telescoping conservation law, and the inversion formula from solving \eqref{eq:zeta} for $\zeta^*$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Analogy with classical transforms]
The Pandrosion spectral theorem plays the role of Fourier analysis \emph{in the spatial variable} (equispaced starts on a circle), while the Pandrosion--Laplace identity plays the role of Laplace analysis \emph{in the temporal variable} (successive iterates of the orbit).  Together they provide a complete harmonic-analytic framework: the Fourier side recovers the static structure of~$P$ (its coefficients), while the Laplace side captures the dynamic convergence to a root.
\end{remark}


%=====================================================================
\section{The true Pandrosion objects: beyond the logarithmic derivative}
\label{sec:true_pandrosion}
%=====================================================================

The classical logarithmic derivative $P'/P = \sum_{k=1}^d 1/(z - \zeta_k)$ is a standard tool of complex analysis, known since Euler.  Renaming it ``Pandrosion field'' adds terminology but no mathematics.  In this section we define three objects that are \textbf{genuinely novel}: they arise from the multi-start architecture of the Pandrosion iteration and have \emph{no classical analogue}.  They require only the evaluation oracle $z \mapsto P(z)$---no derivatives, no coefficients, no access to individual values $P(z)$ or $P(a)$; only their \emph{ratio}.

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The Pandrosion ratio}
\label{sec:pand_ratio}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{definition}[Pandrosion ratio]
\label{def:pand_ratio}
Let $P \in \CC[z]$ be a polynomial of degree~$d$ and let $a, z \in \CC$ with $P(a) \neq 0$.  The \textbf{Pandrosion ratio} is the scalar
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:pand_ratio}
\boxed{r(a, z) := \frac{P(z)}{P(a)} \in \CC.}
\end{equation}
In the multi-start setting with $R > \rho$, $\alpha = e^{i\pi/d}$, $\omega = e^{2\pi i/d}$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ratio_vector}
r_s := r(R\omega^s,\; R\alpha\omega^s) = \frac{P(R\alpha\omega^s)}{P(R\omega^s)}, \qquad s = 0, 1, \ldots, d-1.
\end{equation}
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}[Distinction from the divided difference]
The classical divided difference $Q(a,z) = (P(z) - P(a))/(z-a)$ is a \emph{polynomial of degree~$d-1$} in $z$, and requires knowing $P(z)$ and $P(a)$ separately.  The Pandrosion ratio $r = P(z)/P(a)$ is a \emph{single complex number}, and the Pandrosion step
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:step_from_ratio}
F = a - \frac{z - a}{r - 1}
\end{equation}
uses \textbf{only}~$r$, never $P(z)$ or $P(a)$ individually.  This is a strictly weaker oracle: a ``ratio oracle'' $\mathcal{O}_r(a,z) = P(z)/P(a)$ reveals less than an evaluation oracle $\mathcal{O}(z) = P(z)$, yet it suffices for root-finding.
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[Factorisation of the ratio]
\label{prop:ratio_factor}
The Pandrosion ratio admits the exact product factorisation
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:ratio_product}
r_s = \prod_{k=1}^d \frac{R\alpha\omega^s - \zeta_k}{R\omega^s - \zeta_k},
\end{equation}
where $\zeta_1, \ldots, \zeta_d$ are the roots of~$P$.  Each factor is a M\"obius transformation in $\zeta_k/R$, so $r_s$ is a \emph{nonlinear, multiplicative} functional of the root positions.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
$P(z) = \prod_k(z - \zeta_k)$, so $P(z)/P(a) = \prod_k (z - \zeta_k)/(a - \zeta_k)$.  Substituting $z = R\alpha\omega^s$, $a = R\omega^s$ gives~\eqref{eq:ratio_product}.
\end{proof}

%---------------------------------------------------------------------
\subsection{The Pandrosion field on the circle}
\label{sec:pand_field}
%---------------------------------------------------------------------

\begin{definition}[Pandrosion field]
\label{def:pand_field}
Let $P$ be a polynomial of degree~$d$ with roots inside the disk $|\zeta_k| \leq \rho$, and let $R > \rho$.  The \textbf{Pandrosion field} is the function on the circle
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:pand_field}
\boxed{\mathcal{R}(\theta) := \frac{P(Re^{i(\theta + \pi/d)})}{P(Re^{i\theta})}, \qquad \theta \in [0, 2\pi).}
\end{equation}
Its restriction to the equispaced lattice $\theta_s = 2\pi s/d$ yields the ratio vector $(r_0, \ldots, r_{d-1})$.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[The Pandrosion field is not $f'/f$]
\label{thm:not_fprimef}
The exact relation between $\mathcal{R}(\theta)$ and the logarithmic derivative is
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:exact_relation}
\mathcal{R}(\theta) = \exp\!\left(\int_\theta^{\theta + \pi/d} \frac{P'}{P}(Re^{i\phi})\,iRe^{i\phi}\,d\phi\right).
\end{equation}
In particular, $\mathcal{R}$ is the \emph{exponential of a contour integral} of $P'/P$ along an arc of length $\pi R/d$.  For finite~$d$, this is a \textbf{nonlinear, global} functional of~$P'/P$---not a pointwise evaluation.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By the fundamental theorem of calculus applied to $\log P$:
\[
